    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4410dd993c36479e87dca129a6db22ed">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6c27d604-fc05-4ffa-a8e1-d56fd214b339'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>School of Materials Science and Engineering, Nanyang Technological University, 50 Nanyang Avenue, Singapore, 639798 Singapore</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yan/Qingyu">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Chen/Hong" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Hong Chen</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-name">Hong Chen</p>
                     <p>Stanford Synchrotron Radiation Lightsource, SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory, Menlo Park, CA, 94025 USA</p>
                     <p>Department of Chemistry, University of California, Berkeley, CA, 94720 USA</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chen/Hong">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zhang/Jing%E2%80%90Ping" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Jing-Ping Zhang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">
                     <p class="author-name">Jing-Ping Zhang</p>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhang/Jing%E2%80%90Ping">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Guo/Yu%E2%80%90Guo" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yu-Guo Guo<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d133624230" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are still confronted with several major challenges, including low energy and power densities, short-term cycle life, and poor low-temperature performance, which severely hinder their practical applications. Here, a high-voltage cathode composed of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F nano-tetraprisms (NVPF-NTP) is proposed to enhance the energy density of SIBs. The prepared NVPF-NTP exhibits two high working plateaux at about 4.01 and 3.60 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with a specific capacity of 127.8 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The energy density of NVPF-NTP reaches up to 486 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than the majority of other cathode materials previously reported for SIBs. Moreover, due to the low strain (≈2.56% volumetric variation) and superior Na transport kinetics in Na intercalation/extraction processes, as demonstrated by in situ X-ray diffraction, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique, and cyclic voltammetry at varied scan rates, the NVPF-NTP shows long-term cycle life, superior low-temperature performance, and outstanding high-rate capabilities. The comparison of Ragone plots further discloses that NVPF-NTP presents the best power performance among the state-of-the-art cathode materials for SIBs. More importantly, when coupled with an Sb-based anode, the fabricated sodium-ion full-cells also exhibit excellent rate and cycling performances, thus providing a preview of their practical application.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201701968-sec-0010">
         
         <p>With the rapidly increasing demand for stationary energy storage, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted extensive attention because of their merits of highly abundant sodium resources in the Earth's crust, low cost, and identical working principle as the dominant lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0001_R_d133624222e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Chem. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci. Sci. Adv.">1</a></span> However, SIBs are still confronted with several challenges, such as low-energy/power densities, short-term cycle life, and poor low-temperature (LT) performance.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0002_R_d133624222e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Rev. Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></span> To solve these issues, one of the main research objectives is to develop advanced cathode materials with superior electrochemical properties,<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0003_R_d133624222e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></span> because Na transport in a crystalline cathode material is considerably more difficult than that in its anode counterparts, most of which can be amorphous, alloying, or conversion materials with high Na-transport/reaction kinetics.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0004_R_d133624222e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a></span> For the cathode materials of SIBs, issues of particular concern are as follows: i) high redox potential during the desodiation/sodiation processes to achieve high output voltage and hence high energy density when used in full cells; ii) fast Na-intercalation/extraction kinetics to ensure outstanding power performance; iii) low lattice strain generated during Na intercalation/extraction to achieve a long-term cycle life; and iv) excellent LT performance to allow worldwide applications, especially in places with cold weather.</p>
         
         <p>Among all the cathode materials for SIBs, polyanion-type phosphate with the crystal structure of a Na-super-ionic conductor (NASICON) is of significant interest due to its stable structure of a 3D framework connected by PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedrons and MO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons (M represents the transition metals with varied valence) units.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0005_R_d133624222e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Energy">5</a></span> As a typical NASICON cathode, sodium vanadium phosphate (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) should be subjected to extensive studies because its crystal lattice allows ultrafast and stable Na intercalation/extraction.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0006_R_d133624222e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci. Nano Lett. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></span> Unfortunately, the working (plateau) voltage of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode is as low as 3.3–3.4 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with a theoretical capacity of 117.6 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, implying its theoretically low energy density of only ≈394 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>. This value is evidently lower than that of commercially representative cathode materials of LIBs (e.g., about 500–530 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> for LiFePO<sub>4</sub> cathode). Thus, a big gap in the practical application of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> in SIBs remains. At present, the primary challenge for the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode may lie in increasing its operating voltage and its energy density thereafter.</p>
         
         <p>An effective strategy to achieve a higher operating voltage for Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is enhancing the ionicity of the anionic framework by introducing highly electronegative anions, such as F<sup>−</sup> (which possesses the highest electronegativity) and O<sup>2−</sup>, into the lattice. After introducing F<sup>−</sup> into the SVP lattice to form the new sodium vanadium fluorophosphate (SVFP), V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds are partly replaced by V<span class="icomoon"></span>F, resulting in higher redox potential with the changes in V valence during Na intercalation/extraction. One type of the SVFP material can be written as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(O<sub>2−2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>F<sub>1+2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>) (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1), which indicates that one-third of the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is replaced by F<sup>−</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup> in various proportions. In the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(O<sub>2−2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>F<sub>1+2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>) series, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F, which has the lowest F content (viz., <i>x</i> = 0), should be the better choice than others, such as the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> at <i>x</i> = 1 (with the highest F content), because Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F provides higher and single V valences of +4. Furthermore, lower F content provides weaker inductive effects, which can facilitate Na diffusion and lower polarization.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0007_R_d133624222e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></span> Hence, we can reasonably believe that Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F is a promising cathode with a high operating voltage for SIBs.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0008_R_d133624222e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Power Sources Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Herein, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F material composed of uniform and carbon-free nano-tetraprisms (NVPF-NTP) was controllably prepared via a hydrothermal method. When used as cathode for SIBs, the as-prepared NVPF-NTP exhibited two high working (discharge) plateaux at about 4.01 and 3.60 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with a specific capacity of 127.8 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which is very close to its theoretical value of 130 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. This finding implies that the energy density of NASICON-type cathode material increased by 23.4% from the 394 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> theoretical value of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to the present 486 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> (actual measured value for the NVPF-NTP), which is higher than virtually all of the available cathode materials for SIBs. In addition, the NVPF-NTP also exhibited outstanding high-rate capabilities, which resulted from its nanoscale particle size and high apparent Na-diffusion coefficients, as disclosed by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) studies at various scan rates. More significantly, in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) tests demonstrated that the volumetric variation of the NVPF crystal lattice during Na-intercalation/extraction processes is as low as ≈2.56%. This low strain characteristic bestows the NVPF-NTP with a long-term cycle life (e.g., about 80.9% capacity retention after 2000 cycles at a high rate of 20 C). Moreover, the NVPF-NTP exhibits superior LT performance (e.g., delivering a capacity of 96.1 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which corresponds to a capacity retention of up to 76.4% at 0.2 C and a low temperature of −25 °C) and superior full-cell performance when coupled with an Sb-CNT anode, which is composed of Sb nanoparticles and carbon nanotubes (CNTs).</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0001">1</a>a shows the powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) pattern of the as-prepared material. The Le Bail refinement of the material converges to a tetragonal unit cell (space group: <i>I</i><sub>4</sub>/mmm) with <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 6.3803 Å and <i>c</i> = 10.6184 Å. The result is consistent with the No. 1535056 (tetragonal Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F) data in the Inorganic Crystal Structure Database, which suggests that the prepared material is mainly Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F, except for a small portion of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> phase with three extremely weak peaks at 2θ of 13.2°, 23.9°, and 31.3° in the PXRD pattern.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0009_R_d133624222e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></span> The inset of Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0001">1</a>a gives the crystal structure of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F, with a framework that is composed of VO<sub>5</sub>F octahedrons and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedrons. Meanwhile, VO<sub>5</sub>F octahedrons are connected with PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedrons by sharing O atoms in the <i>ab</i>-plane, and pairs of VO<sub>5</sub>F octahedrons share corner F atoms along the <i>c</i>-direction, finally forming pseudolayers along the <i>ab</i>-plane. The locations between pseudolayers provide sites for Na storage and transport. Hence, the pathways for Na migration in the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F lattice are 2D channels, which favor rapid Na intercalation/extraction during the charge and discharge processes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201701968-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5aa70d28-2895-41ab-adc7-f23456eb3629/adma201701968-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5aa70d28-2895-41ab-adc7-f23456eb3629/adma201701968-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7780a371-c72a-4ed9-ba16-0410042d0fa0/adma201701968-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5aa70d28-2895-41ab-adc7-f23456eb3629/adma201701968-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201701968-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201701968" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural and morphology characterizations of NVPF-NTP. a) PXRD pattern and Le Bail refinements for NVPF-NTP (red dots: observed data; gray line: calculated data; green line: difference curve; blue bars: Bragg position). Inset: Schematic of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F crystal structure. b,c) FTIR (b) and high-resolution V 2p XPS spectrum (c) of NVPF-NTP. d) SEM image of the prepared NVPF-NTP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0001">1</a>b displays the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectrum of the prepared Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F. The broad band at 1000–1150 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be ascribed to the asymmetric stretching vibration (<i>v</i><sub>as</sub>) of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> tetrahedrons, and the bands at 667 and 557 cm<sup>−1</sup> indicate the P<span class="icomoon"></span>O symmetric stretching and bending vibrations (<i>v</i><sub>ss</sub> and <i>v</i><sub>b</sub>), respectively. The V<span class="icomoon"></span>F vibration observed at the weak band of 954 cm<sup>−1</sup> suggests the presence of V<span class="icomoon"></span>F bonds, while the strong band centered at 913 cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the V<span class="icomoon"></span>O stretching vibration.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0010_R_d133624222e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Solid State Sci.">10</a></span> Evidently, the absorbance of V<span class="icomoon"></span>F bonds is markedly weaker than that of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds because of the different absorbance nature of chemical bonds in the FTIR response and the lower number of V<span class="icomoon"></span>F bonds than that of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds in the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F lattice. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was implemented to confirm the V valence state further. In the high-resolution V 2p spectrum (Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0001">1</a>d), the sharp peak centered at 516.7 eV corresponds to the electrons in V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, and another broad peak at the higher binding energy of 523.8 eV is attributed to the electrons in V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. Both peaks are characteristic of V<sup>4+</sup> species and coincide well with a previous report[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0008_R_d133624222e930" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Power Sources Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></span>] and the above PXRD tests. The morphology and size of the prepared Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F were examined by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0001">1</a>d) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Figure S1, Supporting Information). As disclosed, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F is composed of very uniform nano-tetraprisms with a length and width of about 400 and 200 nm, respectively, demonstrating that the above abbreviation of “NVPF-NTP” appropriately denotes the prepared Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F material. The elemental energy dispersive X-ray mapping on SEM (Figure S2, Supporting Information) displayed that Na, O, P, V, and F are homogeneously distributed in the whole sample.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To clarify the Na intercalation/extraction processes of the NVPF-NTPs as a cathode material for SIBs, the galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves and CV were first investigated in the coin-type half-cells with metallic Na plates as both counter and reference electrodes. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0002">2</a>a shows the GCD profiles cycled at 0.1 C (1 C corresponds to a current density of 130 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) over a potential window of 2.0–4.3 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The discharge specific capacity was ≈127.8 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which is very close to the theoretical value of 130 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Two pairs of charge/discharge plateaus were clearly observed above 3.4 V in the profiles. The oxidation/reduction potentials appeared at 4.025/4.007 and 3.632/3.595 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, suggesting extremely low polarizations (18 and 37 mV for the upper and lower plateaus, respectively) for both Na intercalation/extraction reactions. The ratio between the two plateaus was ≈1:1, and the specific capacity of each plateau was ≈63.9 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which corresponds to one Na uptake. The CV tests performed in the same potential window of 2.0–4.3 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were further used to clarify the phase transformation and electrochemical reactions during the Na intercalation/extraction processes. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0002">2</a>b, the NVPF-NTP also exhibits two couples of well-defined anodic/cathodic peaks at 4.061/3.936 and 3.667/3.521 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, which are consistent with the GCD test results. The corresponding two-step desodiation/sodiation reactions can be written as the following electrochemical Equations <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma201701968-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a> and <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma201701968-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201701968-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/018fa187-0b72-4cba-ae05-4f5fa1b88a91/adma201701968-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201701968:adma201701968-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201701968:adma201701968-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma201701968-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/51a31f09-7a58-4e7d-92b5-9aa79b2177d1/adma201701968-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201701968:adma201701968-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201701968:adma201701968-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201701968-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/57e81d96-7351-40fe-bb24-e314dd9e65d2/adma201701968-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/57e81d96-7351-40fe-bb24-e314dd9e65d2/adma201701968-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0db62b0b-a678-487c-8e95-322c734c5499/adma201701968-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/57e81d96-7351-40fe-bb24-e314dd9e65d2/adma201701968-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201701968-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201701968" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural evolutions during Na-intercalation/extraction of NVPF-NTP. a,b) Representative galvanostatic charge/discharge (a) and CV curves of initial cycles (b) for the prepared NVPF-NTP. The rate for galvanostatic test is 0.1 C, CV scan rate is 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, and both potential windows are 2.0–4.3 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. c) The in situ XRD patterns collected during the 1st cycle of NVPF-NTP electrode under a 0.1 C rate with sampling interval of 30 min. d) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curve of the 1st cycle.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Each electrochemical reaction involves one Na uptake/release, and Equations <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma201701968-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a> and <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma201701968-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a> correspond to the lower and upper plateaus, respectively. Moreover, we rationally inferred from the almost overlapped GCD and CV profiles of the initial cycles that all desodiation/sodiation processes are highly reversible, implying its structural stability during Na intercalation/extraction and outstanding cycling performance. This finding is further verified by the crystal structure and morphology tests after cycling (Figure S3, Supporting Information), in situ XRD and other electrochemical tests.</p>
         
         <p>To elucidate the evolutions of the NVPF-NTP crystal structure during Na intercalation/extraction, in situ XRD test with a sampling interval of 30 min was implemented for the 1st cycle at 0.1 C. The obtained in situ XRD patterns are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0002">2</a>c,d; and Figure S4 (Supporting Information). Figure S4a (Supporting Information) shows the full-scale XRD patterns collected during the cycling processes of NVPF-NTP electrode. For the pristine electrode, the XRD pattern was similar to that of the prepared NVPF-NTP powder, except for the diffraction peaks of Al foil (current collector in the cells). Details of the structural evolutions during cycling were monitored via the shifts/changes of selected peaks of (101), (002), (200), (103), and (202) (Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0002">2</a>c). For the diffraction of (200) planes, the peak at 27.997° weakened gradually and then split with the formation of a new peak at the higher 2θ angle of ≈28.219° in the first charge plateau. This peak response corresponds to the two-phase transformation from Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F to conductive Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0011_R_d133624222e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></span> At the end of this plateau, the peak of 27.997° vanished completely along with the appearance of one new diffraction peak at 28.361°. During the second charge plateau, the newly generated peak shifted to the direction of high 2θ angle, and finally merged with peak (103), which always moves to the direction of low 2θ angle in the desodiation (charging) process. When fully charged to 4.3 V, the XRD pattern can be well-indexed to pure-phase NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F, a two-Na-extracted product of NVPF-NTP.[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0003_R_d133624222e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></span>] As a result, the charging process achieved the two-Na (two-electron) full extraction through the transformation from Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F to Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F and then NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F. More importantly, the XRD patterns can return to the pristine state before charging during the subsequent discharging (Na re-intercalation) process, demonstrating that the transformations between the three compounds are completely reversible. These highly reversible variations were also observed from another couple of adjacent peaks of (101) and (002), as well as the isolated (202) peak. Figure S4b,c (Supporting Information) further gives the changes of <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> values in the unit cells upon charging, respectively. The figures show that <i>a</i> values decreased continuously with the Na extraction from crystal lattice, originating from the decreasing cationic radius of the transition metal V because of its increased valence; whereas the <i>c</i> values increased due to the reduction of Na ions, thereby increasing the repulsive force between the pseudolayers.[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0003_R_d133624222e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></span>] Furthermore, by calculating the volume of unit cells, we found that the change in the NVPF-NTP unit cell volume was as low as 2.56%, indicating the extremely low lattice strain of NVPF-NTP during Na intercalation/extraction. It can be inferred that the prepared NVPF-NTP will exhibit an ultrastable cycling performance when used as a cathode material for SIBs due to the high reversibility and low strain of the crystal lattice.</p>
         
         <p>CV at various scan rates and GITT tests were employed to analyze the Na-migration kinetics of the NVPF-NTP electrode to preliminarily evaluate the possibility of high-rate capabilities. CV curves (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0003">3</a>a) were recorded in the potential window of 2.0–4.3 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0003">3</a>b shows the linear fitting profiles between the peak current densities (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and the square root of scan rates (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>). The excellent fitting results and high <i>R</i> values suggest that the electrochemical Na-intercalation/extraction reactions are diffusion-controlled processes. Therefore, the apparent Na-diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) is calculated according to the Randles–Sevcik equation,<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0012_R_d133624222e1146" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Electrochim. Acta">12</a></span> and the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values are 7.47 × 10<sup>−11</sup>, 3.08 × 10<sup>−11</sup>, 3.67 × 10<sup>−11</sup>, and 1.66 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> for peaks A1, C1, A2, and C2, respectively. Evidently, the low-potential A2/C2 redox couple yields smaller <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values than those of the high-potential couple, suggesting that Na intercalation/extraction at a low potential of around 3.5 V is relatively sluggish and requires higher energy.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201701968-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c7405077-98b3-4730-ad8c-d61a6930e6ee/adma201701968-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c7405077-98b3-4730-ad8c-d61a6930e6ee/adma201701968-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7c73d087-2258-4308-bf85-6e98a5133383/adma201701968-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c7405077-98b3-4730-ad8c-d61a6930e6ee/adma201701968-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201701968-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201701968" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Studies of Na-migration kinetics for the NVPF-NTP electrode. a) CV profiles at various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Linear fitting for the relationship between <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> and <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> from CV profiles. c) GITT test results showing the changes of <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> and quasiequilibrium potentials along with the GCD processes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>GITT was tested at a rate of 0.05 C within the potential range of 2.0–4.3 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in the first cycle. For single titration (Figure S5a,c, Supporting Information), a beeline behavior is usually observed between the cell voltage <i>E</i> and the square root of galvanostatic time (τ<sup>1/2</sup>), as shown in Figure S5b,d (Supporting Information). Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0003">3</a>c shows the evolution of the quasiequilibrium potential and calculated <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values along with the GCD curves. Notably, each quasiequilibrium potential (the red squares in Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0003">3</a>c) is extremely close to the GCD curves with potential differences of &lt;20 mV, which is among the smallest values in the previously reported results.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0013_R_d133624222e1211" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a></span> This finding indicates that both Na intercalation/extraction processes are kinetically fast with high ionic and electronic conductivities. The <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values calculated from the GITT curves are in the 10<sup>−13</sup>–10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> order of magnitude, and the values at the upper plateau are evidently higher than those at the lower plateau in both the charge and discharge processes. This finding is consistent with the CV test results. In addition, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values in the middle of each plateau are clearly smaller than those at both ends, which is in agreement with previous reports on other electrode materials.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0014_R_d133624222e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a></span> Notably, the presently calculated <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> values of NVPF-NTP are higher than those of other polyanion-type phosphate cathodes for SIBs,<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0014_R_d133624222e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a>, <a href="#adma201701968-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0015_R_d133624222e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - RSC Adv.">15</a></span> which may be attributed to its nanoscale particle size and 1D morphology.</p>
         
         <p>As discussed above, the NVPF-NTP was predicted to possess superior rate capabilities and a long-term cycle life when used as a cathode material for SIBs due to the superior electrode kinetics and low lattice strain during Na intercalation/extraction. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>a shows the rate capabilities of NVPF-NTP in the half-cells cycled from 0.1 to 40 C. It still delivers a specific capacity of ≈84.1 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high rate of up to 40 C (completing the charge or discharge in only 90 s). This value corresponds to a capacity retention of ≈66% compared to the specific capacity at 0.1 C, indicating that only 34% of the specific capacity is cut back even though the charge/discharge rate increases 400 times from 0.1 to 40 C. The discharge curves at different rates (inset of Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>a) disclose a clear discharge plateau of above 3.0 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na even at the high rate of 40 C, although the two plateaus almost merged into one. This advanced high-rate performance is attributed to the superior kinetics of the NVPF-NTP electrode, which guarantees rapid Na intercalation/extraction during cycling at ultrahigh rates. Furthermore, the specific capacity at 0.1 C can well restore to its initial values after the rate tests of 55 cycles, implying that the NVPF-NTP can work at arbitrarily varied current densities. This outcome promotes the capability of NVPF-NTP to store unstable and intermittent energy sources, such as wind and solar energies. Note that, there is an increasing trend of specific capacity when the cycling is back to 0.1 C after the rate tests. This may be due to the accumulation of large polarization cycled at the high current rate, which needs an appropriate time to achieve the capacity recovery.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201701968-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca7892d4-3489-4b97-93e6-dc15f6e7eedb/adma201701968-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca7892d4-3489-4b97-93e6-dc15f6e7eedb/adma201701968-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc28dd29-3b83-47e0-a60c-24523b437761/adma201701968-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca7892d4-3489-4b97-93e6-dc15f6e7eedb/adma201701968-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201701968-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201701968" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Electrochemical properties of NVPF-NTP in half cells: a) Rate capabilities from 0.1 C to 40 C and the corresponding GCD curves (inset); b) The cycle stabilities at different rates of 1 C for 1000 cycles and 20 C for 2000 cycles. c,d) The low-temperature performance of NVPF-NTP: c) galvanostatic discharge curves of NVPF-NTP cycled at 0.2 C in the temperature range from 25 to −25 °C; d) The capacity retentions along with temperature variations between 25 and −25 °C at various rates of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C. e) The performance of Sb-CNT//NVPF-NTP full cell: rate capabilities of at different rates from 1 to 20 C and the GCD curves of initial 3 cycles at 1 C (left inset). Right inset in (e) shows the application of full cell, powering 33 LED bulbs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The NVPF-NTP also exhibits outstanding cycling performance at different rates of 0.2, 1, and 20 C. After 200 cycles at a relatively low rate of 0.2 C (Figure S6, Supporting Information), the NVPF-NTP achieves a capacity retention of above 93.7% with the capacity decay from the initial 125.6 to 117.7 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. When cycled at 1 C (Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>b), the NVPF-NTP delivers an initial capacity of 117.6 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and retains up to 88.5% of its capacity after 1000 cycles. To test the much longer cycling stability, NVPF-NTP electrode was further cycled at a markedly higher rate of 20 C and exhibited a capacity retention of above 80.9% even after 2000 cycles. The capacity fading rates per cycle were only 0.0115% and 0.00955% at 1 and 20 C, respectively. Notably, the above NVPF-NTP data are superior to those of previously reported SIB cathode materials prepared without the high-temperature annealing procedure.[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0007_R_d133624222e1274" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></span>] The absence of annealing step significantly cut down the production costs to promote the practical applications of the NVPF-NTP for stationary energy storage.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to the superior high-rate capabilities and long-term cycling stability of the as-prepared NVPF-NTP, it more significantly displays outstanding LT performance. Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>c shows the discharge curves tested under various temperature levels from 25 to −25 °C at 0.2 C. When the temperature is &lt;−15 °C, the lower voltage plateau becomes almost a slope, although the upper voltage plateau is still very clear. This should be associated with the more sluggish Na-transport kinetics at the lower voltage plateau than the upper one, viz., the smaller <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> value of the lower voltage plateau as determined by the above CV and GITT tests. Nevertheless, all the curves give two evident plateaus/slopes even at the LT of −25 °C with a specific capacity of 96.1 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (about 76.4% capacity retention compared to that at 25 °C), implying the superior LT kinetics of the NVPF-NTP electrode. Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>d further illustrates the capacity retentions at different discharge rates (0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C) and testing temperatures (from 25 to −25 °C at 10 °C intervals). The NVPF-NTP exhibits excellent LT performance at various charge/discharge rates. For example, the capacity ratios at −25 °C compared to 25 °C are above 76% and 44% at 0.2 and 5 C, respectively. The obtained result is one of the most outstanding LT performances compared with the previously reported SIB cathode materials, except for the almost equivalent data of the Prussian blue/CNT cathode material that we recently published.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0016_R_d133624222e1290" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, sodium-ion full batteries fabricated via coupling the NVPF-NTP cathode with an Sb-CNT anode were tested to preliminarily demonstrate the practicability of the as-prepared NVPF-NTP as a superior cathode for SIBs. Figures S7 and S8 (Supporting Information) give the crystal structure, morphology, and electrochemical performance of Sb-CNT anode, indicating the representative properties with the previously reported Sb-based materials.[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0004_R_d133624222e1294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>, <a href="#adma201701968-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0017_R_d133624222e1294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Mater. Chem. A">17</a></span>] The left inset in Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>e shows the charge and discharge curves of the Sb-CNT//NVPF-NTP full cells at 1 C (the C rate is also calculated based on the NVPF-NTP cathode) in the voltage window of 1.5–3.9 V, clearly disclosing the existence of two well-defined plateaus at ≈3.25 and 2.75 V. All curves are overlapping well, suggesting the excellent cycling stability of the fabricated Sb-CNT//NVPF-NTP full cells. The rate profiles from 1 to 20 C shown in Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>e further discloses the superior rate capabilities of the as-fabricated Sb-CNT//NVPF-NTP full cells. The specific capacity at 1 C is around 120.3 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which is almost the same as that of half cells. Even at a high rate of 20 C (completing the charge/discharge in 3 min), the cell still has a capacity retention of ≈54% compared with the specific capacity at 1 C. As shown in Figure S9 (Supporting Information) the Sb-CNT//NVPF-NTP full cells also exhibit good cycling performance with a capacity retention of more than 80% after 50 cycles at 5 C. The right inset in Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0004">4</a>e demonstrates that one coin-type full cell can power 33 light-emitting diode (LED) bulbs, arranged as “SIB” letters.</p>
         
         <p>According to the above test results, the superior properties of NVPF-NTP cathode include high working potential, outstanding rate and cycling performance, and excellent LT properties. Among these advantages, high working potential should be one of the most significant parameters to fabricate high-energy SIBs. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0005">5</a>a shows the comparison profiles between the present NVPF-NTP and the vast majority of major cathode materials previously reported for SIBs, disclosing the relationship among the average discharge potential, specific capacity, and energy density. Notably, all data were calculated from the reported (present) data of studied electrode materials rather than from those of full cells. The NVPF-NTP possesses a high energy density of almost 500 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is notably higher than those of the majority of reported ones.<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0018_R_d133624222e1315" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span> The Na intercalation/extraction potential of the NVPF-NTP is considerably higher than that of the oxides of P2/P3/O3-Na<sub>0.76</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0018_R_d133624222e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span>] and Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>,[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0018_R_d133624222e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span>] as well as Na<sub>0.61</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.94</sub>[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0018_R_d133624222e1348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span>] cathode materials. However, its energy density is almost equivalent to those of the P2/P3/O3-Na<sub>0.76</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and is slightly lower than that of the Na<sub>0.61</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.94</sub>. The high Na intercalation/extraction potential of a cathode material implies a high working voltage when assembled as sodium-ion full cells, which guarantees substantially simpler series and parallel connections of cells for pack applications. In Figure <a href="#adma201701968-fig-0005">5</a>b, we further compare the Ragone plots (the relevance of energy and power densities) between these state-of-the-art cathode materials<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0018_R_d133624222e1377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span> and the presently prepared NVPF-NTP. As seen in the plots, the NVPF-NTP possesses the best power performance and the highest energy densities at a power density of &gt;100 W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Although the energy densities of the NVPF-NTP are slightly lower than those of the Na<sub>0.61</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.94</sub> and Na<sub>0.76</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> at the power density of &lt;100 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, it exhibits markedly superior power performance over both materials. For example, at the energy density of ≈318 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>, the NVPF-NTP still delivers a power density of almost 10 000 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is about 33 times that with 300 W kg<sup>−1</sup> for the Na<sub>0.61</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.94</sub> cathode material.[<span><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0018_R_d133624222e1416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span>]</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201701968-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd8e1a4a-4c44-4934-a2b1-c3e5c98c9321/adma201701968-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd8e1a4a-4c44-4934-a2b1-c3e5c98c9321/adma201701968-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bfb74fcc-ce24-4e99-be02-7506e4acbfea/adma201701968-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd8e1a4a-4c44-4934-a2b1-c3e5c98c9321/adma201701968-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201701968-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201701968" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The property comparisons between the prepared NVPF-NTP material and the vast majority of key SIBs cathode materials reported: a) Relationships between the average discharge potential, specific capacity, and energy density; and b) the Ragone plots.<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#adma201701968-bib-0018" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma201701968-bib-0018_R_d133624222e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, a superior NVPF-NTP cathode material for SIBs composed of uniform and carbon-free Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F nano-tetraprisms was successfully prepared. Half-cell tests demonstrated that the prepared NVPF-NTP exhibits highly advantageous electrochemical properties in terms of high operating plateaus (4.01 and 3.60 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), outstanding rate capabilities, ultralong cycling stability, and superior LT performance (such as a capacity retention of up to 76.4% with two evident discharge plateaus at −25 °C and 0.2 C). Both GITT and CV at varied scan rates were implemented to demonstrate its superior characteristics in electrode kinetics, and in situ XRD tests disclose the low strain of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F lattice with a volumetric variation of only 2.56% during Na intercalation/extraction processes. On the basis of the advanced electrochemical data, the NVPF-NTP possesses almost the highest material energy density (≈486 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>) and the best power performance among the major cathode materials previously reported for SIBs. When coupled with the anode material, such as Sb-CNT, the fabricated full cells (Sb-CNT//NVPF-NTP) exhibited superior charge/discharge performances. All these results suggest that the as-prepared NVPF-NTP is a promising cathode candidate for SIBs that offers high energy and power densities, ultralong life cycle, and superior low-temperature performance.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
